<div id="anp_a" class="d-panel p" style="display: none"><div class="notes p"><section id="historyarticle-meta1" class="history"><p>Received 2025 Jan 10; Revised 2025 Feb 23; Accepted 2025 Feb 25; Collection date 2025 Mar.</p></section></div></div>
<div id="clp_a" class="d-panel p" style="display: none">

<p>Licensee MDPI, Basel, Switzerland. This article is an open access article distributed under the terms and conditions of the Creative Commons Attribution (CC BY) license (<a href="https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/" class="usa-link usa-link--external" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">https://creativecommons.org/licenses/by/4.0/</a>).</p>
<div class="p"><a href="/about/copyright/" class="usa-link">PMC Copyright notice</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div>PMCID: PMC11902214  PMID: <a href="https://pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/40094815/" class="usa-link">40094815</a>
</div>
</div></section></section><section aria-label="Article content"><section class="body main-article-body"><section class="abstract" id="abstract1"><h2>Abstract</h2>
<p>Hop latent viroid (HLVd), a 256-nucleotide RNA strand with complementary base-pairing and internal stem loop structures, forms circular or rod-shaped molecules within diseased plants. RT-PCR/RT-qPCR was used to assess HLVd transmission, spread and longevity. The viroid was detected in asymptomatic stock plants and in rooted vegetative cuttings, as well as in recirculated nutrient solution sampled from propagation tables and nozzles. Plant-to-plant spread through root infection in hydroponic cultivation was demonstrated. The viroid survived for 7 days and 4 weeks, respectively, in crushed leaf extracts (sap) or dried leaves/roots at room temperature. Following stem inoculation with infectious sap, HLVd was detected in root tissues within 2–3 weeks and in the foliage within 4–6 weeks. Plants grown under a 12:12 h photoperiod to induce inflorescence development showed more rapid spread of HLVd compared to 24 h lighting. The viroid was subsequently detected in inflorescence tissues, in trichome glands, in dried cannabis flowers and in crude resinous oil extracts. Anthers and pollen from infected male plants and seeds from infected female plants contained HLVd, giving rise to up to 100% infected seedlings. Artificially inoculated tomato and tobacco plants supported viroid replication in roots and leaves. Infected cannabis leaf and root tissues treated with UV-C for 3–5 min or temperatures of 70–90 °C for 30 min contained amplifiable HLVd-RNA. Infectious plant extract treated with 5–10% bleach (0.825% NaOCl) or 1000 ppm hypochlorous acid yielded no RT-PCR bands, suggesting the RNA was degraded. Meristem tip culture from HLVd-infected plants yielded a high frequency of pathogen-free plants, depending on the genotype.</p>
<section id="kwd-group1" class="kwd-group"><p><strong>Keywords:</strong> <em>Cannabis sativa</em>, cannabinoids, dudding, marijuana, stunt disease, hop latent viroid</p></section></section><section id="sec1-plants-14-00830"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">1. Introduction</h2>
<p>The commercial cultivation of high Δ9-tetrahydrocannabinol (THC)-containing cannabis (<em>Cannabis sativa</em> L.) plants in Canada and in many US states where it is legally permitted by regulatory jurisdictions is constantly faced with challenges from plant pathogens [<a href="#B1-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1-plants-14-00830">1</a>,<a href="#B2-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2-plants-14-00830">2</a>]. Following the legalization of cannabis use in Canada for medical and recreational purposes in 2018, commercial cultivation has expanded in all regions of the country. Correspondingly, reports of new and emerging pathogen occurrences have increased [<a href="#B1-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1-plants-14-00830">1</a>,<a href="#B2-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2-plants-14-00830">2</a>]. These pathogens are capable of infecting and destroying the roots, stems, foliage and inflorescences of cannabis plants grown under greenhouse conditions and indoor environments, as well as under field conditions [<a href="#B1-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1-plants-14-00830">1</a>,<a href="#B2-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2-plants-14-00830">2</a>,<a href="#B3-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3-plants-14-00830">3</a>,<a href="#B4-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B4-plants-14-00830">4</a>,<a href="#B5-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B5-plants-14-00830">5</a>,<a href="#B6-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B6-plants-14-00830">6</a>,<a href="#B7-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B7-plants-14-00830">7</a>,<a href="#B8-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B8-plants-14-00830">8</a>,<a href="#B9-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B9-plants-14-00830">9</a>,<a href="#B10-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B10-plants-14-00830">10</a>,<a href="#B11-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B11-plants-14-00830">11</a>,<a href="#B12-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B12-plants-14-00830">12</a>,<a href="#B13-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B13-plants-14-00830">13</a>,<a href="#B14-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B14-plants-14-00830">14</a>]. The pathogen pressures are generally greater on greenhouse-grown plants, as they are spaced at high densities and the plants are frequently pruned and handled physically, practices that are known to spread many fungal, bacterial and viral pathogens [<a href="#B15-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B15-plants-14-00830">15</a>]. The commonly used practice of the vegetative propagation of cannabis plants from cuttings derived from stock (mother) plants is also known to spread a number of pathogens [<a href="#B1-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B1-plants-14-00830">1</a>,<a href="#B2-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B2-plants-14-00830">2</a>,<a href="#B3-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B3-plants-14-00830">3</a>,<a href="#B4-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B4-plants-14-00830">4</a>]. Among the range of pathogens reported on cannabis plants, the emergence of hop latent viroid (HLVd), first recorded in 2019–2020 from the USA and Canada, has raised the most significant concern [<a href="#B16-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-plants-14-00830">16</a>]. The rapid spread of the viroid has been shown to result in negative impacts on cannabis plant growth and quality [<a href="#B16-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-plants-14-00830">16</a>]. For example, the viroid causes a reduction in the growth of stems, roots and inflorescences, resulting in stunted plants with poorly developed inflorescences [<a href="#B16-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-plants-14-00830">16</a>]. Viroid infection also reduces the development of stalked glandular trichomes that are the sites of production and storage of a range of cannabinoids and terpene compounds [<a href="#B17-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B17-plants-14-00830">17</a>,<a href="#B18-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B18-plants-14-00830">18</a>]. Consequently, the economic impacts of HLVd are high, and the pathogen is estimated to have caused several billions of dollars in lost revenue to date in North America [<a href="#B19-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B19-plants-14-00830">19</a>]. The losses due to HLVd in other parts of the world are unknown but are likely to be equally significant.</p>
<p>The range of symptoms of cannabis plants infected by HLVd has been recently described [<a href="#B16-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-plants-14-00830">16</a>]. What remains unknown is how the viroid spreads within commercial cannabis production facilities and the extent to which it can survive once introduced. Viroids are the smallest group of pathogens known to affect plants, and are comprised solely of short RNA molecules, 226–430 nucleotides in length [<a href="#B20-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B20-plants-14-00830">20</a>,<a href="#B21-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B21-plants-14-00830">21</a>,<a href="#B22-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B22-plants-14-00830">22</a>,<a href="#B23-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B23-plants-14-00830">23</a>]. The viroids are totally dependent on their host plants for replication since their genetic material is insufficient to encode for any proteins. Various aspects of the biology of these unique pathogens can be found in several recent studies [<a href="#B24-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B24-plants-14-00830">24</a>,<a href="#B25-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B25-plants-14-00830">25</a>,<a href="#B26-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B26-plants-14-00830">26</a>,<a href="#B27-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B27-plants-14-00830">27</a>]. From the perspective of disease management, which is of significant importance to the cannabis industry, several approaches have been described to mitigate the economic impact of viroids on other susceptible agricultural crop species [<a href="#B28-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-plants-14-00830">28</a>,<a href="#B29-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B29-plants-14-00830">29</a>,<a href="#B30-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B30-plants-14-00830">30</a>,<a href="#B31-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B31-plants-14-00830">31</a>]. These include the destruction of diseased plants, the use of sanitizing chemicals to reduce survival and prevent spread, and meristem tip culture to provide pathogen-free planting material [<a href="#B28-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B28-plants-14-00830">28</a>,<a href="#B29-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B29-plants-14-00830">29</a>,<a href="#B30-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B30-plants-14-00830">30</a>,<a href="#B31-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B31-plants-14-00830">31</a>,<a href="#B32-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B32-plants-14-00830">32</a>,<a href="#B33-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B33-plants-14-00830">33</a>,<a href="#B34-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B34-plants-14-00830">34</a>,<a href="#B35-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B35-plants-14-00830">35</a>]. Regarding cannabis, several diagnostic methods have been described recently for HLVd detection (2). However, there have been few studies that provide insight into how the viroid can spread, the impact of sanitizing chemicals on viroid survival (based on the integrity of the RNA molecule that is detectable by PCR amplification), and other aspects of its biology in cannabis plants that can lead to a better understanding of the epidemiology and management of this devastating pathogen.</p>
<p>Therefore, the objectives of this study were to (i) conduct transmission studies to demonstrate the infection of cannabis plants by HLVd; (ii) study the potential mechanisms by which HLVd can spread within and between cannabis plants; (iii) determine the impact of viroid infection on trichome development; (iv) establish the role of pollen and seeds in transmission of HLVd; (v) study the longevity of HLVd in plant tissues under different environmental conditions; (vi) evaluate disinfectants and chemicals that can potentially denature HLVd RNA; and (vii) evaluate meristem tip culture for the potential to eliminate HLVd through tissue culture.</p></section><section id="sec2-plants-14-00830"><h2 class="pmc_sec_title">2. Results</h2>
<section id="sec2dot1-plants-14-00830"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.1. Detection of HLVd in Stock Plants and in Flowering Plants</h3>
<p>Samples of leaf and root tissues obtained from stock plants of 10 cannabis genotypes grown in a designated facility (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>a–c) were subjected to RT-PCR. The results for three genotypes are presented in <a href="#plants-14-00830-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>. They show the viroid was present in leaf samples from the bottom, middle and top of the plant, but not consistently for all genotypes. In some stock plants, the viroid was not detected in the bottom leaves, as shown in <a href="#plants-14-00830-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>e. In other plants, it was not detected in the upper leaves. The most consistent source of tissue in which HLVd was detected in all genotypes tested was the roots. Following RT-PCR, the gels consistently revealed the presence of three bands, with estimated sizes of 256 bp, 512 bp and 768 bp (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>d–f). In some samples, a faint band at 924 bp could also be seen (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>f). The bands were all confirmed to be HLVd through sequence analysis and comparison to various sequences retrieved from GenBank, with 100% sequence similarity.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="plants-14-00830-f001"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 1.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11902214_plants-14-00830-g001.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/0c16/11902214/1865c6c91402/plants-14-00830-g001.jpg" loading="lazy" height="904" width="754" alt="Figure 1"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/plants-14-00830-f001/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Sampling of different tissues from cannabis stock and flowering plants for hop latent viroid. (<strong>a</strong>–<strong>c</strong>) Stock plants of three genotypes were sampled at positions (labelled 1–4) corresponding to roots and leaves from the bottom, middle and top of the canopy. (<strong>d</strong>–<strong>f</strong>) RNA banding patterns following RT-PCR of samples from the plants shown in (<strong>a</strong>–<strong>c</strong>). Multiple bands were observed in all samples except for a sample from the lower position of the plant in (<strong>b</strong>). The band sizes were approx. 256 bp, 512 bp and 768 bp. (<strong>g</strong>) Symptoms of overall stunted growth and reduced inflorescence development due to HLVd infection on a plant of genotype A5 in a flowering room containing trial plants. (<strong>h</strong>) A noninfected plant shows larger overall plant size and inflorescence development. (<strong>i</strong>) Comparison of inflorescence development in a noninfected plant (top) and an HLVd-infected plant (bottom) shows reduced overall size and chlorophyll development due to infection. (<strong>j</strong>,<strong>k</strong>) RT-PCR analysis of flowering stem tissues from 20 randomly selected symptomatic plants within a trial greenhouse compartment show that the presence of a 256 bp size fragment characteristic of HLVd in all plants, while 9 plants showed multiple banding patterns similar to those observed in stock plants.</p></figcaption></figure><p>In flowering plants of one genotype (A5), which was grown within a greenhouse compartment containing trial plants, symptoms of HLVd infection were quite apparent, with visibly stunted growth of affected plants and reduced size of the developing inflorescence stems (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>g–i), symptoms that are consistently observed in HLVd-infected plants [<a href="#B16-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B16-plants-14-00830">16</a>]. Samples of stem tissues from 20 randomly selected symptomatic plants showed that they all contained the 256 bp band, confirming the presence of HLVd. In addition, in nine of these plants, multiple bands similar to those seen in stock plants were also observed (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>j,k).</p></section><section id="sec2dot2-plants-14-00830"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.2. Effect of Ribonuclease Enzymes on HLVd Integrity</h3>
<p>The addition of laboratory-grade RNase A (1–2 units) to purified HLVd RNA extracted from leaf tissues of four cannabis genotypes followed by incubation for 10–20 min at room temperature (21–23 °C) resulted in total degradation and no bands were observed compared to the untreated control (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>a), which showed four bands. By comparison, when these samples were treated with two units of RNase R, two outcomes were observed: the 512 bp band was digested (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>b) or the 512 bp and 768 bp bands (where present) were partially digested and the 256 bp band was reduced in intensity (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>c). These results demonstrate the differential sensitivity of the different-sized RNA bands of HLVd to the activity of RNase A (an endonuclease) and RNase R (an exonuclease) under similar experimental conditions. Based on these findings, together with those from previously published reports [<a href="#B21-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B21-plants-14-00830">21</a>,<a href="#B23-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B23-plants-14-00830">23</a>,<a href="#B25-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B25-plants-14-00830">25</a>,<a href="#B27-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B27-plants-14-00830">27</a>,<a href="#B36-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B36-plants-14-00830">36</a>], the proposed replication steps for HLVd are shown in <a href="#plants-14-00830-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a>d. The circular and linear monomeric forms of 256 bp size showed differential sensitivity to digestion by RNase R, while both the circular and the linear multimeric concatemers forms were digested by RNase A. By adding the enzymes separately to each sample, the circular 256 bp monomeric form could be distinguished from the linear concatemers seen on the RT-PCR gels. The 256 bp bands, however, could comprise a combination of circular and linear forms which could not be distinguished.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="plants-14-00830-f002"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 2.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11902214_plants-14-00830-g002.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/0c16/11902214/6ed7d10766a6/plants-14-00830-g002.jpg" loading="lazy" height="1026" width="791" alt="Figure 2"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/plants-14-00830-f002/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>(<strong>a</strong>) RNA extracted from HLVd-infected plant tissues exposed to ribonuclease A (an endonuclease) followed by RT-PCR shows the complete digestion of the RNA in all lanes (marked by arrows). (<strong>b</strong>) RNA extracted from three infected cannabis genotypes exposed to ribonuclease A and ribonuclease R (an exonuclease) followed by RT-PCR shows complete digestion with ribonuclease A (brown arrows) and digestion of only the 512 bp fragment by ribonuclease R (white arrows). C = control samples without enzymes. (<strong>c</strong>) RNA extracted from five cannabis genotypes (labelled 1–5) exposed to ribonucleases A and R followed by RT-PCR shows complete digestion with ribonuclease A (brown arrows) and digestion of the 512 bp and 768 bp fragments by ribonuclease R (white arrows) for some genotypes. In genotypes 3 and 5, digested with ribonuclease R, the intensity of the 256 bp band was also reduced. C = control samples without enzymes. In all figures, MW = molecular weight standard, +ve = positive control sample infected with hop latent viroid, −ve = water control, A = ribonuclease A, R = ribonuclease R. (<strong>d</strong>) Schematic representation of the replication steps of the hop latent viroid RNA that are subjected to enzymatic degradation. Starting with the circular monomeric form (+ve strand, 256 bp) (A), replication using the host DNA-dependent RNA polymerase II creates a linear −ve strand (Lariat intermediate) to subsequently produce the linear antigenomic concatemers shown in (B). These are converted to linear genomic concatemers of varying sizes, which are digested by a (presumed) ribozyme to give rise to linear monomeric genomic (+) strands shown in (C). The results from enzyme digestion shown in (<strong>a</strong>–<strong>c</strong>) indicate that the circular monomeric form is not degraded by ribonuclease R while the linear concatemers are degraded by both ribonucleases A and R. In addition, the PCR primers detect the circular monomeric form as well as the linear genomic concatemers of varying sizes, giving rise to the multiple bands shown in <a href="#plants-14-00830-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a> and <a href="#plants-14-00830-f002" class="usa-link">Figure 2</a> (adapted from McKernen and Helbert [<a href="#B36-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B36-plants-14-00830">36</a>]).</p></figcaption></figure><p>The secondary structures predicted from the full 256 bp sequence from cannabis genotype ‘M1’ (GenBank accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/OQ420426" class="usa-link" data-ga-action="click_feat_suppl" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">OQ420426</a>) of hop latent viroid as depicted by mfold [<a href="#B37-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link" aria-describedby="B37-plants-14-00830">37</a>] are shown in <a href="#app1-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link">Supplementary Figure S1</a>. The complementary base-pairing and stem loop structures that produce a rod-like shape were determined by the programme algorithm, which calculates the most thermodynamically stable configuration of an RNA sequence by predicting how suboptimal folding may occur and how bases could pair up to minimize free energy. The proposed structures show the complementary base-pairing and the stem loop structures, as well as the secondary structure of the RNA molecule based on different Δ<em>G</em> values (<a href="#app1-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link">Supplementary Figure S1</a>).</p></section><section id="sec2dot3-plants-14-00830"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.3. Transmission of HLVd Through Cuttings from Infected Stock Plants</h3>
<p>Stock plants of genotype ‘S3’ with distinct symptoms of stunting, reduced internode length and reduced leaf size (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>a) were sampled at four positions (roots, bottom leaf, middle leaf and top leaf) and compared to a control plant not displaying these symptoms (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>b). The banding patterns on the RT-PCR gels from these tissue samples (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>c) were identical to those shown in <a href="#plants-14-00830-f001" class="usa-link">Figure 1</a>, consisting of four bands of varying sizes that were absent in the asymptomatic plant. When cuttings obtained from this plant were rooted in rockwool blocks, the emergence of roots and root length were markedly reduced in the HLVd-positive cuttings compared to the HLVd-negative cuttings (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>d,e). Root samples taken at 0, 4, 8 and 12 days following the first root emergence from rockwool blocks of three additional cannabis genotypes (‘T8’, ‘M1’ and ‘B2’) all displayed identical banding patterns consistent with the presence of HLVd. However, in genotype ‘T8’, these bands were only observed at day 12 of root emergence, reflecting a delayed detection of the viroid (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>f). The appearance of the roots on cuttings of the latter three genotypes is shown in <a href="#plants-14-00830-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>g,h. Consistently, all cuttings from HLVd-infected plants showed reduced root emergence and root development (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>g) when compared to the non-infected controls (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>h). On genotype ‘S3’, a pronounced reduction in root development and plant growth resulting from HLVd infection is shown in <a href="#plants-14-00830-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>i. At 14 days after root emergence (28 days after cuttings were obtained), the roots of all cuttings originating from the infected stock plants of four cannabis genotypes (which included the three genotypes shown in <a href="#plants-14-00830-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>f) consistently showed the multiple banding pattern characteristic of HLVd replication (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>j). When a comparison of the infra-red (IR) spectrum of rooted and unrooted cuttings was made (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f003" class="usa-link">Figure 3</a>k), a clear distinction was observed, as the unrooted cuttings displaying a higher leaf surface temperature (24.6 °C) compared to the rooted cuttings (20.3 °C). However, since both sets of cuttings were subsequently found to be HLVd-positive, the IR method was not identifying diseased plants but rather those with a reduced rooting frequency that could also have been due to other causes.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="plants-14-00830-f003"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 3.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11902214_plants-14-00830-g003.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/0c16/11902214/613a2ae6ccde/plants-14-00830-g003.jpg" loading="lazy" height="985" width="744" alt="Figure 3"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/plants-14-00830-f003/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Transmission of hop latent viroid through vegetative cuttings originating from infected stock plants of several cannabis genotypes. (<strong>a</strong>,<strong>b</strong>) Symptomatic and healthy-appearing plants, respectively, of genotype ‘S3’ were sampled at positions marked by 1–4. Symptoms of stunting and shorter internode length are shown in (<strong>a</strong>). (<strong>c</strong>) Banding patterns following RT-PCR of the plants shown in (<strong>a</strong>,<strong>b</strong>) confirms the viroid was present in all tissue samples taken from (<strong>a</strong>) but was absent in all samples taken from (<strong>b</strong>). (<strong>d</strong>,<strong>e</strong>) The extent of root emergence from infected and healthy cuttings in rockwool blocks. On the left side in each photo, cuttings were taken from an infected plant compared to a healthy plant (right). (<strong>f</strong>) Detection of hop latent viroid in roots of cuttings of three cannabis genotypes (G1, G2, G3) infected with HLVd in which the roots had just emerged from the rockwool blocks (time 0) and at various times subsequently (4, 8, 12 days). In genotype G1, the viroid was only detected at day 12 compared to all sampling times in genotypes 2 and 3, suggesting a slower movement of the viroid into the roots had occurred. (<strong>g</strong>,<strong>h</strong>) Root development on cuttings of each of three genotypes shown in (<strong>f</strong>) at 14 days after emergence from the rockwool blocks compared to cuttings from HLVd-infected stock plants (<strong>h</strong>). (<strong>i</strong>) Growth comparison of a cutting from an infected stock plant (left) of genotype ‘G1’ compared to a healthy plant (right) at 3 weeks. (<strong>j</strong>) Banding patterns following RT-PCR of the cuttings shown in (<strong>h</strong>) plus genotype G4, at 14 days after first emergence of roots from the rockwool blocks. All cuttings contained the viroid. In all figures, MW = molecular weight standard; +ve = positive control sample infected with hop latent viroid; −ve = water control. (<strong>k</strong>) Infra-red image analysis conducted on four cannabis cuttings, two rooted and two unrooted (top), shows a higher temperature emittance in the rooted cuttings (24.6 °C) compared to the unrooted cuttings (20.3 °C) (bottom). All four cuttings were positive for HLVd, suggesting the IR image was reflecting greater transpiration from rooted cuttings and not the presence/absence of HLVd.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="sec2dot4-plants-14-00830"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.4. Transmission of HLVd Through Roots of Infected Plants</h3>
<section id="sec2dot4dot1-plants-14-00830"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">2.4.1. Kloner Experiments</h4>
<p>The placement of an HLVd-positive rooted cutting of cannabis genotype ‘B2’ showing distinct symptoms of stunting and reduced root growth (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>a,b) next to an HLVd-negative cutting with large leaves and extensive root development within a Kloner with recirculating nutrient solution for a period of 2 weeks resulted in the transmission of the viroid to the previously healthy plant (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>c). The RT-PCR banding pattern characteristic for HLVd was observed in two replicate samples taken from the previously uninfected plant of ‘B2’. The experiment was repeated with cannabis genotype ‘K4’ with similar results.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="plants-14-00830-f004"><h5 class="obj_head">Figure 4.</h5>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11902214_plants-14-00830-g004.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/0c16/11902214/c992cab7791e/plants-14-00830-g004.jpg" loading="lazy" height="1001" width="704" alt="Figure 4"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/plants-14-00830-f004/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Transmission of hop latent viroid through infected roots of cannabis plants and through recirculated nutrient solution. (<strong>a</strong>) Placement of a HLVd-infected cutting and a healthy cutting of genotype ‘B2’ in the same Kloner. (<strong>b</strong>) Root development on both plants at 2 weeks shows a difference between the infected and healthy plant. (<strong>c</strong>) RT-PCR analysis of plants from the Kloner before and after the start of the experiment. Results are shown for duplicate plants (P1, P2). No bands were observed in the healthy plants before inoculation and bands reflecting positive detection of HLVd were observed in the roots of these plants after 2 weeks. (<strong>d</strong>) Placement of a HLVd-infected plant of ‘B2’ with healthy plants at the opposite ends of a tray. The trays were flooded twice daily for 2 weeks, after which plants were sampled. (<strong>e</strong>) Underside of the rockwool block showing development of roots in the infected ‘B2’ plant used in the experiment. (<strong>f</strong>) RT-PCR results from analysis of healthy and infected plants used in the experiment, water (nutrient) sample, and roots and leaves from the previously healthy plants which now contain the viroid. (<strong>g</strong>) A sample of nutrient solution from around the root zone shows the presence of small broken roots and sloughed off root cells. (<strong>h</strong>,<strong>i</strong>) Detection of HLVd (as denoted by +ve) using RT-qPCR. The viroid was present on the root surface, on the surface of the table immediately below the roots, in the grooves of the table where irrigation water accumulated, in drainage water and in the irrigation nozzles releasing recirculated nutrient solution.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="sec2dot4dot2-plants-14-00830"><h4 class="pmc_sec_title">2.4.2. Greenhouse Experiments</h4>
<p>HLVd-infected plants of ‘S3’ and ‘B2’ were placed on a tray distal to asymptomatic healthy plants of the same genotypes for a 2-week period and subjected to daily flooding (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>d). The cuttings were initiated in rockwool blocks and the emerging roots were visible on the bottom (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>e). In three healthy plants, no bands were obtained following RT-PCR analysis of root samples at the start of the experiment (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>f). By comparison, root samples from two infected plants at the start of the experiment confirmed the presence of HLVd. Water samples taken from the flooding treatment showed faint bands of 256 bp size that corresponded to HLVd (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>f, lanes 6,7). A microscopic examination of these water samples showed they contained broken root segments and sloughed-off roots cells (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>g). Subsequently, root and leaf samples from the previously healthy ‘B2’ plants subjected to flooding showed the presence of HLVd in these tissues after two weeks of exposure to water, confirming the transmission of the viroid (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>f, lanes 8–12). The experiment was repeated once with similar results.</p></section></section><section id="sec2dot5-plants-14-00830"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.5. Distribution of HLVd in a Trial Greenhouse Environment</h3>
<p>Samples of crushed roots and swabs obtained from the surface of roots of symptomatic plants presumed to be infected with HLVd in a trial greenhouse, as well as swabs taken of the surface of propagation tables, irrigation nozzles, and samples of pooled water on tables and recirculated nutrient solution, were assayed for the presence of HLVd using RT-qPCR. The results from duplicate samples are presented in <a href="#plants-14-00830-t001" class="usa-link">Table 1</a>. At least one sample from each location was positive for HLVd, with low Ct values (reflecting higher viroid levels) observed in crushed roots and in swabs taken from the surface of tables underneath the rockwool blocks (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f004" class="usa-link">Figure 4</a>h,i). Samples below a Ct value of 33 were considered positive for HLVd (<a href="#plants-14-00830-t001" class="usa-link">Table 1</a>). To correlate these Ct values to approximate viroid copy numbers, a dilution curve was established using synthetic viroid clones of known concentrations of HLVd (<a href="#app1-plants-14-00830" class="usa-link">Supplementary Figure S2</a>). A series of control dilutions was made using synthetic HLVd in nuclease-free water and subjected to the same PCR conditions as the samples that were mixed with plant tissues. These latter samples were used to determine the limits of detection by adding 100 μL of each serial dilution to 900 μL of homogenized tissue (100 mg of stem tissues) from an HLVd-negative plant, followed by PCR. The results showed that approximately 100 to 10,000 copies of HLVd were present on the surface of greenhouse benches. The limit of detection was determined at a Ct value of 33, or 10 copies of the viroid.</p>

<div class="tw-foot p"><div class="fn" id="fn2"><p><sup>a</sup> Presence/absence of HLVd was based on a Ct cut-off value of 33 or lower.</p></div></div></section></section><section id="sec2dot6-plants-14-00830"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.6. Transmission of HLVd by Mechanical Inoculation</h3>
<p>Inoculations were made of rooted cuttings of five cannabis genotypes that were grown in Kloners for 4 weeks under 24 h of light (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f005" class="usa-link">Figure 5</a>a,b) by exposing the surface of a cut stem to infectious sap (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f005" class="usa-link">Figure 5</a>c–e) followed by sampling for HLVd at 4 weeks and 6 weeks post-inoculation. RT-PCR analysis of four replicate samples showed that plants were free of HLVd at the start of the experiment (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f005" class="usa-link">Figure 5</a>f). Multiple banding patterns characteristic of HLVd were observed in the inoculated plants (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f005" class="usa-link">Figure 5</a>g).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="plants-14-00830-f005"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 5.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11902214_plants-14-00830-g005.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/0c16/11902214/6bcaa65834c5/plants-14-00830-g005.jpg" loading="lazy" height="1000" width="721" alt="Figure 5"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/plants-14-00830-f005/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>Transmission of hop latent viroid through stem inoculations conducted on hydroponically grown plants in Kloners. (<strong>a</strong>) Individual cuttings are inserted into Styrofoam holders and held in place and allowed to root. (<strong>b</strong>) After 4 weeks of growth, individuals stems on each plant (shown by arrows) were cut and discarded. (<strong>c</strong>) The exposed cut stem surface (arrow) with a drop of exudate. (<strong>d</strong>) Sap extracted from leaves of an HLVd-infected plant was dabbed onto the exposed surface of the cut stem using a Q-tip. (<strong>e</strong>) Two weeks after inoculation, new shoot growth can be seen on either side of the cut stem (arrow). (<strong>f</strong>) Results from RT-PCR of stem cuttings used in the experiment, showing the absence of HLVd in all cuttings used. (<strong>g</strong>) RT-PCR conducted on root (R) and leaf (L) tissues of four genotypes inoculated 4 weeks prior with HLVd shows the presence of the viroid in all root samples (blue arrows) and in three out of seven leaf samples. Multiple bands are indicative of active replication of the viroid. (<strong>h</strong>) Results from RT-PCR conducted 6 weeks after stem inoculation of four genotypes of cannabis (PK, BC, PWE, Mac) shows the presence of the viroid in all tissue samples (roots and leaves).</p></figcaption></figure><p>At 4 weeks after inoculation, all root and some leaf samples (four out of seven) from four genotypes, with from two to four replicates of each, were positive for HLVd (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f005" class="usa-link">Figure 5</a>g). After 6 weeks, consistent banding patterns were observed in all leaf and root samples (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f005" class="usa-link">Figure 5</a>h), indicating successful transmission of HLVd had occurred in all four genotypes.</p>
<p>Additional inoculations were made on larger plants (6 weeks of age) of genotypes ‘S3’ and ‘B2’ on cut stem surfaces (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f006" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>a) and these plants were grown for an additional 5 weeks under 24 h of light. Root, leaf and petiole samples taken from various positions around the plants at 2 and 5 weeks after inoculation were subjected to RT-PCR. At 2 weeks, the viroid was absent in the roots and leaves of uninoculated plants (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f006" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>b) but was detected in the roots and in the uppermost leaves of inoculated plants (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f006" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>c), and was absent in other parts of the plant. At 5 weeks after inoculation, when the plants had grown considerably larger (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f006" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>d), HLVd was detected in all root and petiole samples, but was absent in one leaf sample taken from the top of the plant (position 4) and was faintly detected in one leaf sample taken from the bottom of the plant (position 5) (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f006" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>e). The multiple banding pattern characteristic of HLVd was observed in all tissues. In most samples, petiole tissues showed more intense bands compared to leaf tissues, suggesting a higher accumulation of the viroid. A schematic representation of the spread of HLVd following stem inoculation is shown in <a href="#plants-14-00830-f006" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>f. The viroid was first detected in the roots, followed by spread to the upper leaves and then to the lower leaves of the plant. At 10 weeks after inoculation, these plants developed symptoms of HLVd infection, which included detection in the roots, followed by spread to the upper leaves and then the lower leaves of the plant. At 10 weeks after inoculation, these plants developed symptoms of HLVd infection, which included smaller leaves and less vigorous growth (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f006" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>g). The mechanical inoculation of leaves of cannabis cuttings that included exposing physically macerated leaf lamina to infectious sap (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f006" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>h) or introducing sap to the cut ends of leaves (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f006" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>i) showed transmission in the former but not the latter (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f006" class="usa-link">Figure 6</a>j).</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="plants-14-00830-f006"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 6.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11902214_plants-14-00830-g006.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/0c16/11902214/fa2a26e5a5c3/plants-14-00830-g006.jpg" loading="lazy" height="1062" width="735" alt="Figure 6"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/plants-14-00830-f006/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>(<strong>a</strong>) Inoculation of a 6-week-old plant of genotype ‘S3’ on a cut stem surface (arrow) shows the locations (1–6) where samples were taken prior to inoculation and after 2 weeks. (<strong>b</strong>) RT-PCR of tissues sampled before inoculation shows the plant was HLVd-negative. (<strong>c</strong>) RT-PCR of tissues sampled 2 weeks after inoculation shows HLVd is present in the youngest leaves (1) and in the roots (6). (<strong>d</strong>) The same plant shown in (<strong>a</strong>) was sampled 5 weeks after inoculation at positions (1–6). (<strong>e</strong>) RT-PCR analysis shows the viroid was present at various sampling positions in the foliage and in the roots. The multiple bands characteristic of HLVd can be seen. In many samples, petiole tissues showed more intense bands compared to leaf tissues. (<strong>f</strong>) Schematic representation of the spread of HLVd from stem inoculations to various parts of the plant, with detection in the roots (1) followed by detection in young leaves (2) and then in the middle and lower leaves (3). (<strong>g</strong>) Symptoms on a plant inoculated on a cut stem surface and grown for 10 weeks (left) compared to an uninoculated control plant (right). The leaves are reduced in size and the overall growth is less vigorous. (<strong>h</strong>) Physical abrasion of leaves that resulted in tearing at positions marked (1, 2, 3) followed by HLVd sap inoculation was compared to the cutting of leaf edges with scissors shown in (<strong>i</strong>), and then sap inoculated at positions marked (1*, 2*, 3*). (<strong>j</strong>) RT-PCR analysis of leaves from inoculated plants wounded as shown in (<strong>h</strong>) revealed the presence of HLVd in three leaf samples while plants wounded as shown in (<strong>i</strong>) did not become infected, as shown by the absence of bands corresponding to HLVd.</p></figcaption></figure></section><section id="sec2dot7-plants-14-00830"><h3 class="pmc_sec_title">2.7. Effect of a 12:12 H Photoperiod on Viroid Spread</h3>
<p>The transfer of one group of 4-week-old plants of genotype ‘H6’ displaying no symptoms of infection but with positive detection of HLVd in the roots (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f007" class="usa-link">Figure 7</a>a) from a 24 h photoperiod to a 12:12 h photoperiod in a growth chamber induced inflorescence development when compared to plants that were maintained under constant 24 h light (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f007" class="usa-link">Figure 7</a>b). The infected flowering plants were shorter in stature and more compact than the infected vegetative plants. Plants of genotypes ‘K4’, ‘M1’ and ‘S3’, grown under 24 h of light and then stem-inoculated 24 h prior to transfer to a 12:12 h photoperiod, showed a similar transition to flowering. Root, lower leaf, mid-leaf and flower samples taken 21 days later were compared for HLVd presence to those of plants similarly inoculated but kept under constant 24 h light. The plants grown under a 12:12 h photoperiod showed the characteristic multiple banding pattern for HLVd in all tissues sampled (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f007" class="usa-link">Figure 7</a>c), while those under the 24 h light regime showed viroid presence in the roots, with faint bands in the upper leaves after 3 weeks (<a href="#plants-14-00830-f007" class="usa-link">Figure 7</a>c). RT-qPCR data from samples taken at 12 and 21 days following transfer to the different photoperiods for genotypes ‘M1’, ‘K4’ and ‘S3’ are shown in <a href="#plants-14-00830-t002" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>. In all tissues sampled from the plants grown under a 12:12 h photoperiod, the Ct values were lower (viroid levels were higher) in the three genotypes compared to constant 24 h light (<a href="#plants-14-00830-t002" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>). When comparing tissue types, the top leaves had the highest viroid levels compared to mid-leaves and roots. Where flowers were present (in the 12:12 h photoperiod), viroid levels were the highest in these tissues compared to all other tissues (<a href="#plants-14-00830-t002" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>). There were also differences between the genotypes in the levels of viroid present, with genotype ‘S3’ (considered to be highly susceptible to HLVd) showing the highest levels, followed by genotype ‘M1’ (highly susceptible) and lastly genotype ‘K4’ (moderately susceptible). However, since these represent single Ct values from nonreplicated samples, they infer trends of genotype susceptibility without statistical analysis.</p>
<figure class="fig xbox font-sm" id="plants-14-00830-f007"><h4 class="obj_head">Figure 7.</h4>
<p class="img-box line-height-none margin-x-neg-2 tablet:margin-x-0 text-center"><a class="tileshop" target="_blank" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/core/lw/2.0/html/tileshop_pmc/tileshop_pmc_inline.html?title=Click%20on%20image%20to%20zoom&amp;p=PMC3&amp;id=11902214_plants-14-00830-g007.jpg"><img class="graphic zoom-in" src="https://cdn.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/blobs/0c16/11902214/b48203e835ef/plants-14-00830-g007.jpg" loading="lazy" height="1185" width="704" alt="Figure 7"></a></p>
<div class="p text-right font-secondary"><a href="figure/plants-14-00830-f007/" class="usa-link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Open in a new tab</a></div>
<figcaption><p>The transfer of one set of 4-week-old HLVd-positive plants of genotype ‘H6’ displaying no symptoms of infection (<strong>a</strong>) from a 24 h photoperiod to a 12:12 h photoperiod in a growth chamber promoted inflorescence development when compared to plants maintained under constant 24 h light (<strong>b</strong>). The flowering plants were shorter in stature and more compact than the vegetative plants. When the experiment was repeated using artificially inoculated plants of genotypes ‘K4’, ‘M1’ and ‘S3’, inoculated 24 h prior to the transfer to a 12:12 h photoperiod, a similar transition to flowering was observed. When the roots, lower leaves, mid-leaves and flowers were sampled 21 days later and compared to plants kept under constant 24 h light for HLVd presence, the difference was significant (<strong>c</strong>). All tissues sampled from the plants grown under a 12:12 h photoperiod showed the characteristic multiple banding pattern for HLVd, while those with 24 h light showed the viroid was present in the roots, while faint bands could be seen in the leaves (<strong>c</strong>). RT-qPCR data from samples taken at 12 and 21 days following transfer to the different photoperiods for genotypes ‘M1’, ‘K4’ and ‘S3’ are shown in <a href="#plants-14-00830-t002" class="usa-link">Table 2</a>. When the inoculation experiment was repeated with an autoflower genotype, in which the plants produce inflorescences at a certain growth stage regardless of photoperiod, the transition from vegetative growth (<strong>d</strong>) to flowering was observed in both sets of plants—those placed under the 12:12 h photoperiod and constant 24 h light (<strong>e</strong>). The former plants visually appeared to have a slightly more advanced inflorescence development. The RT-PCR results from tissues taken from these two sets of plants showed similar banding patterns, with a predominant band of 256 bp size observed in all tissues (roots, leaves, flowers) and at both photoperiods (12:12 and 24 h). The root samples had a more intense band overall compared to the rest of the plant (<strong>f</strong>).</p></figcaption></figure><section class="tw xbox font-sm" id="plants-14-00830-t002"><h4 class="obj_head">Table 2.</h4>
<div class="caption p"><p>Effect of two different photoperiods on the distribution and levels of Hop latent viroid in various tissues of three cannabis genotypes, as determined by RT-qPCR.</p></div>
